        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'e8a40b20-0a81-439a-a703-4a3b947b7a04'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d59636072" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The emerging sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are one of the most promising candidates expected to complement lithium-ion batteries and diversify the battery market. However, the exploitation of cathode materials with high-rate performance and long-cycle stability for SIBs has remained one of the major challenges. To this end, an efficient approach to enhance rate and cycling performance by introducing an ordered bicontinuous porous structure into cathode materials of SIBs is demonstrated. Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) are selected because they are recognized as a type of most promising SIB cathode materials. Thanks to the presence of 3D continuous channels enabling fast Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion as well as the intrinsic mechanical stability of bicontinuous architecture, the resultant PBAs exhibit excellent rate capability (80 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 2.5 A g<sup>1</sup>) and ultralong cycling life (&gt;3000 circulations at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>), reaching the top performance of the reported PBA-based cathode materials. This study opens a new avenue for boosting sluggish ion diffusion kinetics in electrodes of rechargeable batteries and also provides a new paradigm for solving the dilemma that electrodes failure due to high-stress concentration upon ion storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202402005-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202402005-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The application of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in large-scale energy storage systems has been hampered considerably by the rising costs and unnerving distribution of lithium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0001_R_d59635936e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0004_R_d59635936e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with LIBs, environmentally friendly, low-cost, and intrinsically safe sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted great attention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0005_R_d59635936e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Rev.">5</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0008_R_d59635936e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, one of the crucial problems of SIBs is the lack of cathode materials with high-rate capability and long cycle life, because the large sodium ions usually lead to slow electrochemical kinetics and structural destruction of cathode materials during their intercalation/deintercalation processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0009_R_d59635936e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Energy">9</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0011_R_d59635936e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, most of available SIB cathode materials are bulk nonporous or porous candidates with discontinuous ion channels, which hinder the smooth and fast ion diffusion inside the cathodes and thus limit the rate capability of SIBs seriously.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0012_R_d59635936e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0015_R_d59635936e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The introduction of 3D continuous channels may ensure smooth and fast diffusion of the electrolyte across the whole material, which, however, has been rarely achieved thus far.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0016_R_d59635936e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0019_R_d59635936e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Sci. Adv.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Recently, bicontinuous porous structures have appealed tremendous interest owing to their intrinsic 3D continuous channels and frameworks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0020_R_d59635936e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Chem.">20</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0022_R_d59635936e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In general, ordered bicontinuous structures have specific symmetries in crystallographic space groups, such as double primitive (DP, <i>Im</i><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/aa15c9ba-92ee-4ae9-96e3-98ae5742d19a/adma202402005-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202402005:adma202402005-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202402005-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo></mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math><i>m</i>) and its single primitive derivative (SP, <i>Pm</i><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/bce98791-1181-45bf-a6c6-ffcb37cec788/adma202402005-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202402005:adma202402005-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202402005-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>3</mn>
                        <mo></mo>
                     </mover>
                     <mi>m</mi>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}m$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0023_R_d59635936e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0025_R_d59635936e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These structures have proven to show excellent capability to release mechanical stress and strain, thus their functional materials are recognized as mechanical metamaterials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0026_R_d59635936e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">26</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0028_R_d59635936e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Science">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Introducing these architectures into cathode materials of SIBs may provide an opportunity to solve the aforementioned problems. However, because of the complex curved channels, these structrures have been generally called as plumber's nightmare structures;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0029_R_d59635936e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Mater.">29</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0031_R_d59635936e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> it is very difficult to artificially construct functional materials with these structures by both bottom-up (such as self-assembly) and top-down strategies (e.g., etching or lithography), particularly for those with pore sizes in the range of several to hundreds of nanometers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0029_R_d59635936e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Mater.">29</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0031_R_d59635936e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To our knowledge, there have been no reports on the application of bicontinuous porous materials as the electrodes of SIBs thus far. In particular, the effect of mechanical properties of bicontinuous structures on cycling stability of energy storage devices has remained unexplored. These vacancies inspire the interest to study the effect of bicontinuous structures on the rate and cycling performance of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0032_R_d59635936e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In the present study, we chose Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) as a candidate because they are considered as one of the most competitive commercial cathode materials for SIBs thanks to their environmental friendliness and high discharge voltages (associated with high energy densities).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0033_R_d59635936e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Commun.">33</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0036_R_d59635936e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Their generic chemical formula can be expressed as A<i><sub>x</sub></i>M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i> <sub>y</sub><i>n</i>H<sub>2</sub>O (with 0 &lt; <i>x</i>  2, 0 &lt; <i>y</i>  1), where A, M, and  represent the alkali metal (Li, Na, K, etc.), transition metal (Fe, Mn, Co, Ni, Cu, etc.), and the vacancies occupied by the coordinated water, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0037_R_d59635936e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nature">37</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0040_R_d59635936e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Mater. Horiz.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, it is highly difficult to control over the structures of PBAs including pore shapes, because in the conventional coprecipitation synthetic method, the nucleation and grain growth of PBAs usually occur very fast, which is hard to control and results in random aggregation of irregular PBA particles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0041_R_d59635936e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Mater. Chem. A">41</a>-<a href="#adma202402005-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0045_R_d59635936e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Small">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, to introduce a bicontinuous (plumber's nightmare) structure into PBAs, we adopted polymer cubosomes (PCs, generally defined as discrete colloidal particles of bicontinuous cubic liquid crystalline phases) as a template and a vapor-diffusion technique (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202402005-fig-0001">1</a>). This approach allows a very slow diffusion of the vapor of one PBA precursor into the open channels of the PC template, which then reacts with the other precursor existed in the channels. The method significantly reduces the nucleation and grain growth rates of PBAs, thus achieving excellent structural control. The resultant PBAs (SP-PBAs) inherit the cubosome morphology and have an ordered SP bicontinuous topology with a uniform pore diameter of 68&nbsp;nm and a specific surface area (SSA) of 45&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>. Serving as an SIB cathode, SP-PBA displays excellent rate performance (80 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at a current density of 2.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>) and ultralong cycling life (&gt;3000 circulations at 0.5 A g<sup>1</sup>, and the capacity retention is 79.3%); the average capacity reduction per 100 cycles is only 0.69%. This outstanding performance ranks in the top metrics of all the reported state-of-the-art PBA-based cathode materials. The mechanisms for the high-rate and cycling stability, profiting from the bicontinuous structure, were revealed, which would help the design and preparation of high-performance electrode materials for energy storage devices.</p>

            <figure class="figure" id="adma202402005-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1ab6f9dd-faa0-4add-9e6e-df5fc01d9f63/adma202402005-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1ab6f9dd-faa0-4add-9e6e-df5fc01d9f63/adma202402005-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9ae6f1c4-8501-49dd-8e5c-de9cdd4b8c90/adma202402005-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1ab6f9dd-faa0-4add-9e6e-df5fc01d9f63/adma202402005-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202402005-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202402005" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic preparation of the SP-PBA cubosomes with an SP bicontinuous structure templated by PCs, which were prepared by self-assembly of PS-<i>b</i>-PEO in solution. The PS blocks constituted the framework of PC, while the hydrophilic PEO chains constructed the surrounding coronae, which localized in the open channels. After the precursor [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4</sup> filled in the open channel, HCl gas slowly diffused into the channel and reacted with [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4</sup> to form continuous PBA phase. SP-PBA was then obtained after removing the PS-<i>b</i>-PEO template. In the inset showing a unit cell of SP-PBA, the PBA microscopic crystal structure consists of a face-cubic phase, in which Fe1 is C-coordinated while Fe2 is N-coordinated.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202402005-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202402005-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202402005-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202402005-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials Characterizations</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">PCs were obtained by self-assembly of a polystyrene<sub>220</sub>-<i>block</i>-poly(ethylene oxide)<sub>45</sub> (PS<sub>220</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>) copolymer in solution, according to our previously reported procedures;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0021_R_d59635936e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202402005-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0046_R_d59635936e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the details are given in the Supporting information (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1S5</a>, Supporting Information). The PCs with continuous open channels inside make them an perfect template for the fabrication of functional materials with a plumber's nightmare structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0047_R_d59635936e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nat. Commun.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The precursors of PBAs can be filled into the exposed channels and react inside to form continuous PBA phase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0001">1</a>). In the present study, the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i> PBA system was employed and the reaction steps are listed below

                                       </msub>

                              </msup>
                              <mo></mo>
                              <mi>Fe</mi>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close="]" open="[" separators="">
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi>Fe</mi>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                             <mi>CN</mi>
                                          </mfenced>
                                          <mn>6</mn>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mo></mo>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{F}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}^{3 + }} + \,{{\left[ {{\mathrm{Fe}}{{{\left( {{\mathrm{CN}}} \right)}}_{\mathrm{6}}}} \right]}^{4 - }} \rightleftharpoons {\mathrm{Fe}}{{\left[ {{\mathrm{Fe}}{{{\left( {{\mathrm{CN}}} \right)}}_{\mathrm{6}}}} \right]}^ - }\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>In this reaction, [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4</sup> and HCl are precursor reactants. The decomposition of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4</sup> can be triggered by H<sup>+</sup> of HCl to generate CN<sup></sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0034_R_d59635936e874" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">34</a>, <a href="#adma202402005-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0048_R_d59635936e874" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Mater. Chem. A">48</a>, <a href="#adma202402005-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0049_R_d59635936e874" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to make these reactants diffuse into the open channels of the PC template before they form PBA particles with sizes larger than the diameter of the channel, the reaction speed should be slowed down sufficiently. To this aim, we adopted an HCl vapor-diffusion technique, which involves two steps (see Scheme <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). First, the solution of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4</sup> filled into the open channel of PCs driven by the capillary force, and then the filled PCs were collected by suction filtration. Second, the slow vapor diffusion of HCl into the mesochannels filled with the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4</sup> solution significantly reduced the reaction speed. This means could reduce the defects in the formed PBA crystals, which is conducive to the improvement of their electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0045_R_d59635936e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Small">45</a>, <a href="#adma202402005-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0050_R_d59635936e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Energy Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, as the reaction continued in the pores, the nuclei grew up and slowly formed the 3D continuous PBA networks in the PC template. Compared with the traditional solution coprecipitation method, our method can minimize the amount of the PBA precursors remaining outside the PC template and thus improve the purity of SP-PBAs. After 48-h HCl vapor-diffusion and continuous reaction, the PC template was removed by dissolution in tetrahydrofuran (THF), yielding SP-PBAs. In contrast, the traditional solution co-precipitation method is highly difficult to prepare a considerable amount of pure bicontinuous-structured samples due to the fast reaction of the PBA precursors outside the PC template (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), mainly yielding mesoporous-free cube PBA particles (denoted as MF-PBA, see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a>, Supporting Information). In addition to MF-PBA, PBA particles with disordered and discontinuous mesoporous (DM-PBA) were also synthesized as another control sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b,c</a>, the synthetic details are given in the Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0051_R_d59635936e906" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The elimination of the polymer template is demonstrated by Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). In the FTIR spectra of SP-PBA and the control samples, the peaks at 501 cm<sup>-1</sup> are associated with the vibration mode of FeC and the characteristic peaks located at 2067 cm<sup>1</sup> correspond to the CN stretching vibration. In addition, the broad peaks at 3428 cm<sup>1</sup> and the weak peaks at 1612 cm<sup>1</sup> can be attributed to the HOH bending vibration of the coordinated/interstitial H<sub>2</sub>O and the OH stretching vibration, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0011_R_d59635936e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results indicate the formation of Fe-based PBAs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0052_R_d59635936e931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The face-centered cubic phase with an <i>Fm</i><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2faa48fb-503b-4965-b2b5-a326142babed/adma202402005-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202402005:adma202402005-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202402005-math-0006.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>3</mn>
                        <mo></mo>
                     </mover>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math><i>m</i> space group (JCPDS 00-001-0239) for the three samples is revealed by X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202402005-fig-0002">2a</a>), which is a typical crystal structure of PBAs. The X-ray photoelectron spectrum (XPS) of SP-PBAs shows that a little less amount of Fe<sup>2+</sup> ions have been converted to Fe<sup>3+</sup> compared with those in the two control samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0048_R_d59635936e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Mater. Chem. A">48</a>, <a href="#adma202402005-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0053_R_d59635936e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) and inductively coupled plasma (ICP) were carried out to measure the water and element contents in the three samples; the results are given in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0042_R_d59635936e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Environ. Sci.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a consequence, the chemical formula for the DM-PBA, MF-PBA, and SP-PBA samples are determined to be Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.774</sub>1.94H<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>0.87</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.921</sub>1.65H<sub>2</sub>O, and Na<sub>0.95</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.949</sub>1.93H<sub>2</sub>O, respectively. The vacancy contents of the three samples are given in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202402005-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b7221532-2c30-4cdf-a9ef-053dba9067a1/adma202402005-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b7221532-2c30-4cdf-a9ef-053dba9067a1/adma202402005-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aef183ce-cb66-4636-a7cc-b5ab249120da/adma202402005-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b7221532-2c30-4cdf-a9ef-053dba9067a1/adma202402005-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202402005-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202402005" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of DM-PBA, MF-PBA, and SP-PBA (JCPDS 00-001-0239). b) SEM image of the SP-PBA cubosomes. c) SEM image of fractured SP-PBA with an inset of a locally magnified view. <i>L</i><sub>p</sub> is the side length of a cubic unit. d,e) TEM images of SP-PBAs. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c,d</a> (Supporting Information) exhibits the histograms of the frame and pore diameter distributions. f) HRTEM image; the inset is the IFFT pattern. g) SAXS pattern of dried SP-PBA powder (<i>a</i> = 2/<i>q</i><sup>*</sup>). h) HAADF micrograph and the corresponding EDS mapping images of the Fe, C, N, and Na elements.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The SEM images of SP-PBA are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0002">2b</a> and those of the DM-PBA, MF-PBA samples are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a,b</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. SP-PBA displays an ordered porous spherical structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information) and the mean diameter is 2.6  0.7&nbsp;m (as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a>, Supporting Information), which is similar to that of PCs. Moreover, the regional view of SP-PBA distinctly exhibits their internal porous structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0002">2c</a>); the inset SEM image shows a cubic structure of each unit cell along the (100) direction. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c,d</a> (Supporting Information), SP-PBA displays an average pore diameter of 67&nbsp;nm with a uniform frame thickness of 36&nbsp;nm, which approximates to the pore size (34&nbsp;nm) of PCs. The mean side length of the cubic units is <i>L</i><sub>p</sub> = 103&nbsp;nm, similar to that of the PC template (<i>L</i><sub>t</sub> = 100&nbsp;nm, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3d</a>, Supporting Information). TEM images of SP-PBA confirm the ordered spherical porous structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0002">2d,e</a>). The lattice fringes of PBA are clearly observed in the HRTEM image of SP-PBA (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0002">2f</a>); the inset shows the magnified and the inverse fast Fourier transmission (IFFT) pattern, the lattice fringe with adjacent <i>d</i>-spacing of 2.6 is attributed to the (400) atomic plane of PBA. Based on the N<sub>2</sub> adsorptiondesorption isotherms (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), the SSAs of DM-PBA, MF-PBA, and SP-PBA are calculated, by the BrunauerEmmettTeller (BET) method, to be 163.8 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information), 2.1 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c</a>, Supporting Information), and 45.0 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12e</a>, Supporting Information), respectively. Moreover, an average pore diameter of SP-PBA, obtained by the BarrettJoynerHalenda (BJH) method, is about 68&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12f</a>, Supporting Information), which is similar to the results of SEM. The average pore size of DM-PBA is around 5&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information), whereas MF-PBA does not show porous characteristics (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12d</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0002">2g</a> shows the SAXS pattern of SP-PBAs, which confirms their SP topology with a <i>Pm</i><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f6b3df33-1a3b-4274-90e0-58c8e4015de3/adma202402005-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202402005:adma202402005-math-0007" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202402005-math-0007.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>3</mn>
                        <mo></mo>
                     </mover>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math><i>m</i> symmetry by showing typical peaks with a <i>q</i> ratio of 1:<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/485d7536-f71f-464d-8691-fe2b661c7333/adma202402005-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202402005:adma202402005-math-0008" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202402005-math-0008.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <msqrt>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                        </msqrt>
                        <mo>:</mo>
                        <msqrt>
                           <mn>3</mn>
                        </msqrt>
                        <mo>:</mo>
                        <msqrt>
                           <mn>4</mn>
                        </msqrt>
                        <mo>:</mo>
                        <msqrt>
                           <mn>5</mn>
                        </msqrt>
                        <mo>:</mo>
                        <msqrt>
                           <mn>6</mn>

                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\sqrt 2 :\sqrt 3 :\sqrt 4 :\sqrt 5 :\sqrt 6 $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>. The unit cell parameter (<i>a</i>) of SP-PBA is determined to be <i>a</i> = 103&nbsp;nm, indicating a precise nanocasting. The high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) micrographs confirm the internal ordered porous structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0002">2h</a>). The corresponding energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping images show the homogeneous distributions of the Na, Fe, C, and N elements in an SP-PBA cubosome.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202402005-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202402005-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>SP-PBA was used as the cathode material of SIBs. The electrochemical performances of SP-PBA and the control samples were evaluated between 2.0 and 4.2&nbsp;V using a NaClO<sub>4</sub>-based electrolyte. In the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of SP-PBA at 0.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>1</sup> (as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), three couples of oxidation/reduction peaks near 2.9, 3.4, and 3.8&nbsp;V can be attributed to the three different Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites at 8c (body-center), 24d (face-center), and 32f (shifted from the 8c sites toward the N-coordinated corner), respectively, in the cubic framework of PBA crystal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0054_R_d59635936e1176" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Phys. Chem. C">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The highly coincident CV curves of SP-PBA exhibit the reversibility of the chargedischarge process. DM-PBA only displays one pair of reversible redox peaks near 3.0&nbsp;V, suggesting a low specific capacity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with SP-PBA, the redox process of MF-PBA is less reversible, and its CV curves of the second and the third cycles show obvious capacity fading (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14c</a>, Supporting Information). At 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup>, SP-PBA displays a reversible discharge capacity of 120.6 and 119.9 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at the second and tenth cycles, respectively, which are corresponding to the reversible CV curves (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202402005-fig-0003">3a</a>). Meanwhile, the polarization of SP-PBA in the CV curves does not increase significantly with the increase of the cycling number. In contrast, DM-PBA shows much lower capacity of 48.5 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at the tenth cycle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a>, Supporting Information). For MF-PBA, the initial cycles capacity attenuation during charging/discharging processes is attributable to the increase of the polarization (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14d</a>, Supporting Information). The SP-PBA cathode exhibits a specific discharge capacity of 118.6 mAh g<sup>1</sup> after 50 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0003">3b</a>), which is higher than those of DM-PBA (46.5 mAh g<sup>1</sup>) and MF-PBA (90.1 mAh g<sup>1</sup>). Moreover, the rate performances of SP-PBA, MF-PBA and DM-PBA were evaluated at different current densities of 252500&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0003">3c</a>). SP-PBA delivers specific capacities of 119.1, 114.6, 103.0, 99.5, 92.6, and 86.1 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 25, 50, 250, 100, 500, and 1000&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup>, respectively. Even under a high current density of 2500&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup>, a high discharge capacity of 80 mAh g<sup>1</sup> still retains, which is much higher than those of DM-PBA (1 mAh g<sup>1</sup>) and MF-PBA (1 mAh g<sup>1</sup>). The capacity can recover to 117 mAh g<sup>1</sup> when the current density reduces to 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup>, revealing the high reversibility of the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. The introduction of bicontinuous porous structure can bring high specific surface area, high porosity, and more binding sites, which are conducive to enhancing the surface/interface adsorption rates and electrochemical reaction kinetics. This feature may afford the electrode materials with bicontinuous structures enormous prospect in the field of fast charging. The coulombic efficiencies of the three samples at current density of 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup> and rate were given in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). SP-PBA exhibits high coulombic efficiency of 100%. The long-term cycle life is highly challenging for SIBs, due to the structural distortion of the cathode materials caused by Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. In our study, SP-PBA exhibits a remarkable capacity of 70.6 mAh g<sup>1</sup> after 3000 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0003">3d</a>). A high capacity retention of 85.7% after 1500 cycles and even 79.3% after 3000 cycles can be achieved, corresponding to a capacity attenuation rate of only 0.69% per 100 cycles. Moreover, after 3000 cycles, the bicontinuous structure and crystalline state of the cycled SP-PBA were characterized by SEM, EDS, and XRD (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Nearly original structure retains, indicating excellent structural stability of SP-PBA. In stark contrast, the MF-PBA structure breaks or collapses after only 300 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). The mechanisms for the obvious difference in the cycling stability will be discussed in the following texts. To our knowledge, such excellent rate performance and long-cycle life rank in the top metrics of all the reported PBA-based cathode materials of sodium-ion batteries (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202402005-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/30357bef-1ad4-41a7-9c01-76a37c652973/adma202402005-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/30357bef-1ad4-41a7-9c01-76a37c652973/adma202402005-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a63bbaaf-64b9-4730-9369-af7dc51c3737/adma202402005-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/30357bef-1ad4-41a7-9c01-76a37c652973/adma202402005-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202402005-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202402005" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The sodium storage performance. a) Galvanostatic charge and discharge curves of SP-PBA. b) Cycling performance of SP-PBA, MF-PBA, and DM-PBA at 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup>. c) Rate performance of SP-PBA, MF-PBA, and DM-PBA. d) Long cycling performance of SP-PBA and MF-PBA at 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup>. e) GITT profiles of SP-PBA. f) Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusivities of SP-PBA, MF-PBA, and DM-PBA in the discharge process, calculated by GITT testing. g) EIS plots and equivalent circuit fitting results of SP-PBA, MF-PBA, and DM-PBA; the inset shows the equivalent circuit model.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To investigate the origin of the significantly enhanced rate performance of SP-PBA, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) testing and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurement were applied to compare the diffusion kinetics of the Na<sup>+</sup> ions in SP-PBA, MF-PBA, and DM-PBA. In the GITT testing, SP-PBA, MF-PBA and DM-PBA display discharge capacities of 118.9, 116.7, and 61.5 mAh g<sup>1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0003">3e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18a,b</a>, Supporting Information). The mean Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) of the three samples are calculated (the details are given in the Supporting Information, and a single GITT step of SP-PBA was presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). In the discharge process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0003">3f</a>), the SP-PBA sample exhibits a much higher <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> (1.04  10<sup>10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup>) than those of DM-PBA (4.6  10<sup>11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup>) and MF-PBA (2.7  10<sup>11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup>), reflecting that the bicontinuous structure may help the electrolyte to diffuse smoothly into the deep interior area of SP-PBA.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0046_R_d59635936e1342" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusivities of the three samples during the charging process are also given in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18c</a> (Supporting Information). In comparison, in DM-PBA the discontinuous mesoporous lead to the obstruction of the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion into the internal portion, resulting in a reduced <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>. In MF-PBA lack of porous structure, Na<sup>+</sup> ions have to pass through a long distance inside the bulk PBA to contact with the internal active sites, leading to a much smaller <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information, for schematic illustration). In the EIS plots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0003">3g</a>), SP-PBA displays a smaller semicircle compared to those of MF-PBA and DM-PBA, implying a lower charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) induced by the bicontinuous porous structure. To further compare <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the three samples, an equivalent circuit model is employed to fit the EIS plot (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0003">3g</a>). The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of SP-PBA, MF-PBA, and DM-PBA are calculated to be 140, 658, and 434 , respectively. The much lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the SP-PBA electrode indicates that the continuous PBA conductive framework can provide an ordered highway for electron transport. Meanwhile, SP-PBA has higher electrical conductivity (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), probably because the fewer vacancy defects and the introduction of a 3D ordered continuous framework allow the electrons to move in an ordered way on the frame. All these results indicate that the introduction of bicontinuous porous structure can improve the Na<sup>+</sup> solid-state diffusion kinetics in PBA, which contribute majorly to the excellent rate capability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202402005-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202402005-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Mechanisms</h3>
            
            <p>In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) technique was performed to analyze the structural evolution of SP-PBA with the initial cubic phase in the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion processes. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202402005-fig-0004">4a</a> displays an in situ XRD pattern of SP-PBA during the first three cycles in the voltage range of 2.04.2&nbsp;V at the current density of 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup>. Four representative reflections of the (200), (220), (400), and (420) planes are illustrated, in which peak shifts and intensity changes are observed without the appearance of new peaks. The result indicates that the sodium storage in SP-PBA only involves a quasi-solid solution reaction mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0055_R_d59635936e1413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Adv. Energy Mater.">55</a>, <a href="#adma202402005-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0056_R_d59635936e1413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at 38.6 is attributed to the aluminum current collector that remains invariable in the whole discharge/charge process. In the first charging process, the cubic phase structure does not show any changes before charged to 3.2&nbsp;V, and then the characteristic peaks slightly shift back to lower angles when continuously charged to 4.2&nbsp;V. This phenomenon can be attributed to a cubic to tetragonal phase transition because of more Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation, in which the crystal lattice is expanded. Prior to discharging back to 3&nbsp;V, the reversible peak shift is attributed to the transition from the tetragonal to the cubic phase, and the subsequent transition from the cubic to the rhombohedral phase is ascribed to the further insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> to 2.0&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0057_R_d59635936e1423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Nat. Commun.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The three-phase transitions of the SP-PBA cathode were demonstrated during the first cycle, and the discharge/charging process of SP-PBA is schematically presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information). During the subsequent discharge cycle, the four peaks gradually shift back to the original diffraction angle and the inverse change is observed, suggesting that the de/sodiation reactions are highly reversible. We also tested the ex situ XRD patterns of DM-PBA and MF-PBA samples in different states during the first charge and discharge process, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information). PBA possess large interstitial voids (&gt;3.5 ), which can accommodate a large number of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>r</i> = 1.02 ). Meanwhile, the coordination-bonded rigid framework is intrinsically resistive to the structural lattice parameter variation induced by full de/intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0052_R_d59635936e1441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">52</a>, <a href="#adma202402005-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0058_R_d59635936e1441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Environ. Sci.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0004">4c</a> shows the changes in lattice parameters and the cell volume. Notably, SP-PBA exhibits a very small lattice parameter variation (1.9%) and cell volume expansion (5.7%) in the de/sodiation reaction, which are smaller than those of most reported cathode materials of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0059_R_d59635936e1450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv. Energy Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This difference indicates the excellent structural stability of SP-PBA, which contributes to its long-cycle life as a cathode of SIB.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202402005-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c8772208-43f9-412e-8d67-089a3128515e/adma202402005-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c8772208-43f9-412e-8d67-089a3128515e/adma202402005-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2efb2a1d-9adf-48b0-a81e-b191f4607f55/adma202402005-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c8772208-43f9-412e-8d67-089a3128515e/adma202402005-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202402005-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202402005" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium storage electrochemical mechanism of SP-PBA. a) In situ XRD results and 2D contour images of the (200), (220), (440), and (420) peaks during the first three cycles. b) The corresponding lattice parameter changes and c) the charge/discharge profiles (at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup>). The Dis. indicates the discharge process, and the Cha. indicates the charging process. d) In situ Raman spectrum during the first three cycles and e) the corresponding charge/discharge profiles. f) The 3D contour image of the in situ Raman spectrum in (d).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, in view of the high sensitivity of the CN vibration stretching mode to the oxidation state of the coordinated Fe, In-situ Raman technique was applied to identify the active sites upon Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0060_R_d59635936e1482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Inorg. Chem.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The corresponding first three discharge/charge profiles (at the current density of 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup>) are also displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0004">4e</a>. In the first charging process, the characteristic peak centering at 2160 cm<sup>1</sup>, attributed to the Fe<sup>3+</sup> coordinated CN group, gradually shifts to the higher wavenumbers with an increase in the intensity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0011_R_d59635936e1497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma202402005-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0061_R_d59635936e1497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This result proves the oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup> to Fe<sup>3+</sup>, accompanied with the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. In the reverse process, the main peak at the high wavenumber shifts to a low wavenumber with a gradual decrease of the intensity to zero when discharged to 2&nbsp;V, demonstrating that Fe<sup>3+</sup> is reduced to Fe<sup>2+</sup> during the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation. A similar phenomenon is also observed in the following cycles, confirming a reversible intercalation/deintercalation reaction. The 3D contour image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0004">4f</a> shows the visual reversible Raman intensity changes during the first three charge-discharge processes.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202402005-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202402005-sec-0060-title">2.4 Effect of the Mechanical Properties of the Bicontinuous Structure on the Long-Cycle Life</h3>
            
            <p>Bicontinuous structures have strong capability to release mechanical stress and strain, thus their functional materials are recognized as mechanical metamaterials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0062_R_d59635936e1527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - ACS Nano">62</a>, <a href="#adma202402005-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0063_R_d59635936e1527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Acc. Chem. Res.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to compare the different mechanical properties and deformation mechanisms of our PBA cathodes during sodiation, the von Mises stress profiles of MF-PBA and SP-PBA under isotropic initial stress conditions were simulated by the finite element analysis (FEA) method using the COMSOL software. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202402005-fig-0005">5a,b</a> shows the single cell transient von Mises stresses of MF-PBA and SP-PBA under different degrees of sodiation (from 10% to 100%), respectively. In MF-PBA (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0005">5a</a>), a high strain energy density is concentrated in the face-centered region of the cube unit, a higher level of red color indicates a larger von Mises stresses. As the degree of sodiation increases, the stress concentration area caused by the intercalation of sodium ions into MF-PBA occupies a large portion of the unit volume. In the cycling process, the high-stress concentration area is prone to crack propagation and even catastrophic brittle fracture as shown in the results mentioned above (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), leading to poor cycling stability. From a geometric perspective, the cube has geometric discontinuities at the corners and edges (which can be understood as the function curve is not differentiable at the critical point). In this case, the stress cannot be dispersed evenly, resulting in excessive local stress. The mechanical performance of SP-PBA is quite different. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0005">5b</a> displays the von Mises stress distributions of different sodiation degrees; the results demonstrate that the strain energy density distribution in the bicontinuous porous structure during the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation is much more uniform than that in the cubic solid. The bicontinuous structure is a highly symmetrical geometric shape that is consistent and continuous in any direction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402005-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0027_R_d59635936e1550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Lett.">27</a>, <a href="#adma202402005-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402005-bib-0064_R_d59635936e1550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Nano Lett.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the isotropic periodicity of SP-PBA makes stress evenly and symmetrically distributed on the support struts during charging and discharging, which can be one of the reasons for the high mechanical properties of SP-PBA. In addition, SP-PBA has a smoother and more continuous surface, which means that when an object with a unit volume is acted upon by an external force, the surface of the structure bear more uniform force. Due to the presence of corners and edges, the surface area to volume ratio of a cube is different in different directions, showing anisotropy, so the stress distribution is uneven. Based on the FEA simulation results, we quantified the volume ratio of PBA with a high-stress ratio (80%) during sodiation for the two structures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0005">5c</a>). The high-stress proportion of MF-PBA increases gradually with an increase in the sodiation degree; the highest transient state reaches 30.9% of the total volume. In contrast, SP-PBA exhibits a much lower high-stress proportional volume with an average value of only 1.75%, which remains stable throughout the whole discharge/charging process. From the perspective of the transmission path, MF-PBA is a whole solid, while SP-PBA is a dense skeleton composed of several nodes. MF-PBA might have poorer internal connectivity and can only transmit stress through fewer paths, at which point the stress is more concentrated; SP-PBA has more internal nodes and higher connectivity, and the load can be dispersed through multiple paths, leading to relatively uniform stress distribution. Moreover, we investigated the concentration and flux of sodium ions in SP-PBA at a 50% sodiation degree. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402005-fig-0005">5d</a>, Na<sup>+</sup> ions can be uniformly distributed in the bicontinuous porous structure, leading to a relatively gentle and stable sodium ion flux in SP-PBA. Overall, the bicontinuous porous structure can enable a low high-stress concentration and a uniform stress distribution during the sodium-ion intercalation, which contributes much to the excellent long cycle performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202402005-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/03f3f976-e7d1-4f21-ab37-7b53ed3bbafd/adma202402005-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/03f3f976-e7d1-4f21-ab37-7b53ed3bbafd/adma202402005-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/28d1dd27-24e6-460c-97b5-1a493ae91e50/adma202402005-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/03f3f976-e7d1-4f21-ab37-7b53ed3bbafd/adma202402005-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202402005-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202402005" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Von Mises stress contour plots of the stress of a) MF-PBA and b) SP-PBA with an increase in the degree of sodiation during the sodiation. c) High-stress volume ratio during the sodiation. d) The concentration and flux of the sodium ions in SP-PBA at a 50% sodiation degree. Surface: ion concentration (mol m<sup>3</sup>); Streamline: positive ion flux; Streamline color: electrolyte salt concentration (mol m<sup>3</sup>).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202402005-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202402005-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion&nbsp;</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we prepared PBAs with an ordered SP bicontinuous porous structure. SP-PBA shows uniform, ordered 3D porous arrays with a pore diameter of 68&nbsp;nm. Serving as a cathode for SIBs, SP-PBA showed excellent electrochemical performance with long cycle life (a capacity retention of 79.3% after 3000 cycles, at 0.5 A g<sup>1</sup>) and outstanding rate capability (80 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 2.5 A g<sup>1</sup>). The high-rate performance of SP-PBA was attributed to the bicontinuous porous structure that could significantly shorten the ion migration path, thus promoting the ion diffusion dynamics. The in situ XRD analysis demonstrated the highly reversible three-phase transition mechanism between cubic, rhombohedral and tetragonal during the electrochemical reaction. SP-PBA exhibited a small-volume variation of only 5.7% in the charging/discharging process. The excellent long-term stability of SP-PBA during electrochemical process benefited from the excellent mechanical properties of the bicontinuous porous structure, which enabled low high-stress concentrations and uniform stress distribution. The insightful understanding of the structural effect on rate capability and cycle life of SIB cathodes holds promise to help the design of other high-performance energy storage devices.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="adma202402005-bib-0003"><span class="bullet">3</span><span class="author">E. A. Olivetti</span>, <span class="author">G. Ceder</span>, <span class="author">G. G.
